	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Queen Victoria</h1>

<p><b>Victoria</b> (Alexandrina Victoria; 24 May 1819&#160;– 22 January 1901) was <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_the_United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Queen of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> from 20 June 1837 until her death. On 1 May 1876, she adopted the additional title of <a href="/wiki/Empress_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Empress of India">Empress of India</a>. Known as the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, her reign of 63 years and seven months was <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_in_Britain_by_length_of_reign" title="List of monarchs in Britain by length of reign">longer than that of any of her predecessors</a>. It was a period of industrial, cultural, political, scientific, and military change within the United Kingdom, and was marked by a great expansion of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.
</p><p>Victoria was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Duke_of_Kent_and_Strathearn" title="Prince Edward, Duke of Kent and Strathearn">Prince Edward, Duke of Kent and Strathearn</a> (the fourth son of <a href="/wiki/King_George_III" class="mw-redirect" title="King George III">King George III</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Princess_Victoria_of_Saxe-Coburg-Saalfeld" title="Princess Victoria of Saxe-Coburg-Saalfeld">Princess Victoria of Saxe-Coburg-Saalfeld</a>. After both the Duke and his father died in 1820, she was <a href="/wiki/Kensington_System" title="Kensington System">raised under close supervision</a> by her mother and her <a href="/wiki/Comptroller" title="Comptroller">comptroller</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Conroy" title="John Conroy">John Conroy</a>. She inherited the throne aged 18 after her father's three elder brothers died without surviving legitimate issue. The United Kingdom was an established <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> in which the sovereign held relatively little direct political power. Privately, she attempted to influence government policy and ministerial appointments; publicly, she became a national icon who was identified with strict standards of <a href="/wiki/Victorian_morality" title="Victorian morality">personal morality</a>.
</p><p>Victoria married her cousin <a href="/wiki/Prince_Albert_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a> in 1840. Their <a href="#Issue">children</a> married into royal and noble families across the continent, earning Victoria the <a href="/wiki/Sobriquet" title="Sobriquet">sobriquet</a> "the grandmother of Europe" and spreading <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia_in_European_royalty" title="Haemophilia in European royalty">haemophilia in European royalty</a>. After Albert's death in 1861, Victoria plunged into deep mourning and avoided public appearances. As a result of her seclusion, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Republicanism in the United Kingdom">republicanism in the United Kingdom</a> temporarily gained strength, but in the latter half of her reign, her popularity recovered. Her <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Golden Jubilee of Queen Victoria">Golden</a> and Diamond Jubilees were times of public celebration. She died on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a> in 1901. The last <a href="/wiki/British_monarch" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarch">British monarch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a>, she was succeeded by her son <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Edward VII</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Titles,_styles,_honours_and_arms"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Titles, styles, honours and arms</span></a>

</li>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_family">Birth and family</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Denning,_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Victoria aged 4" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Denning%2C_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Denning%2C_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Denning%2C_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Denning%2C_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Denning%2C_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Denning%2C_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3755" data-file-height="4711" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Denning,_Stephen_Poyntz_-_Princess_Victoria_aged_Four_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Victoria aged four by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Poyntz_Denning" title="Stephen Poyntz Denning">Stephen Poyntz Denning</a>, 1823</div></div></div>
<p>Victoria's father was <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward,_Duke_of_Kent_and_Strathearn" title="Prince Edward, Duke of Kent and Strathearn">Prince Edward, Duke of Kent and Strathearn</a>, the fourth son of the reigning King of the United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/George_III" class="mw-redirect" title="George III">George III</a>. Until 1817, Edward's niece, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Charlotte_of_Wales" title="Princess Charlotte of Wales">Princess Charlotte of Wales</a>, was the only legitimate grandchild of George III. Her death in 1817 precipitated a succession crisis that brought pressure on the Duke of Kent and his unmarried brothers to marry and have children. In 1818 he married <a href="/wiki/Princess_Victoria_of_Saxe-Coburg-Saalfeld" title="Princess Victoria of Saxe-Coburg-Saalfeld">Princess Victoria of Saxe-Coburg-Saalfeld</a>, a widowed German princess with two children—<a href="/wiki/Carl,_3rd_Prince_of_Leiningen" title="Carl, 3rd Prince of Leiningen">Carl</a> (1804–1856) and <a href="/wiki/Princess_Feodora_of_Leiningen" title="Princess Feodora of Leiningen">Feodora</a> (1807–1872)—by her first marriage to the <a href="/wiki/Emich_Carl,_2nd_Prince_of_Leiningen" title="Emich Carl, 2nd Prince of Leiningen">Prince of Leiningen</a>. Her brother <a href="/wiki/Leopold_I_of_Belgium" title="Leopold I of Belgium">Leopold</a> was Princess Charlotte's widower. The Duke and Duchess of Kent's only child, Victoria, was born at 4.15&#160;a.m. on 24<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>May 1819 at <a href="/wiki/Kensington_Palace" title="Kensington Palace">Kensington Palace</a> in London.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victoria was christened privately by the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Manners-Sutton" title="Charles Manners-Sutton">Charles Manners-Sutton</a>, on 24<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>June 1819 in the Cupola Room at Kensington Palace.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> She was baptised <i>Alexandrina</i> after one of her godparents, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I of Russia</a>, and <i>Victoria</i>, after her mother. Additional names proposed by her parents—Georgina (or Georgiana), Charlotte, and Augusta—were dropped on the instructions of Kent's eldest brother <a href="/wiki/George,_Prince_Regent" class="mw-redirect" title="George, Prince Regent">George, Prince Regent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At birth, Victoria was fifth in the line of succession after the four eldest sons of George<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>III: the Prince Regent (later George<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>IV); <a href="/wiki/Frederick,_Duke_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick, Duke of York">Frederick, Duke of York</a>; <a href="/wiki/William,_Duke_of_Clarence" class="mw-redirect" title="William, Duke of Clarence">William, Duke of Clarence</a> (later William<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>IV); and Victoria's father, Edward, Duke of Kent.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The Prince Regent had no surviving children, and the Duke of York had no children; further, both were estranged from their wives, who were both past child-bearing age, so the two eldest brothers were unlikely to have any further legitimate children. The Duke of Clarence and the Duke of Kent married on the same day in 1818, but both of Clarence's legitimate daughters died as infants. The first of these was Princess Charlotte, who was born and died on 27 March 1819, two months before Victoria was born. Victoria's father died in January 1820, when Victoria was less than a year old. A week later her grandfather died and was succeeded by his eldest son as George<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>IV. Victoria was then third in line to the throne after York and Clarence. Clarence's second daughter was <a href="/wiki/Princess_Elizabeth_of_Clarence" title="Princess Elizabeth of Clarence">Princess Elizabeth of Clarence</a> who lived for twelve weeks from 10 December 1820 to 4 March 1821 and, while Elizabeth lived, Victoria was fourth in line.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Duke of York died in 1827. When George<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>IV died in 1830, he was succeeded by his next surviving brother, Clarence, as William<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>IV, and Victoria became <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Regency_Act_1830" title="Regency Act 1830">Regency Act 1830</a> made special provision for Victoria's mother to act as regent in case William died while Victoria was still a minor.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> King William distrusted the Duchess's capacity to be regent, and in 1836 he declared in her presence that he wanted to live until Victoria's 18th birthday, so that a <a href="/wiki/Regency" class="mw-redirect" title="Regency">regency</a> could be avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_presumptive">Heir presumptive</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg/220px-Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg/330px-Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg/440px-Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1344" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Princess_Victoria_and_Dash_by_George_Hayter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Victoria with her spaniel Dash by <a href="/wiki/George_Hayter" title="George Hayter">George Hayter</a>, 1833</div></div></div>
<p>Victoria later described her childhood as "rather melancholy".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Her mother was extremely protective, and Victoria was raised largely isolated from other children under the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Kensington_System" title="Kensington System">Kensington System</a>", an elaborate set of rules and protocols devised by the Duchess and her ambitious and domineering <a href="/wiki/Comptroller" title="Comptroller">comptroller</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Conroy" title="John Conroy">John Conroy</a>, who was rumoured to be the Duchess's lover.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The system prevented the princess from meeting people whom her mother and Conroy deemed undesirable (including most of her father's family), and was designed to render her weak and dependent upon them.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The Duchess avoided the court because she was scandalised by the presence of King William's illegitimate children.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Victoria shared a bedroom with her mother every night, studied with private tutors to a regular timetable, and spent her play-hours with her dolls and her <a href="/wiki/King_Charles_Spaniel" title="King Charles Spaniel">King Charles Spaniel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dash_(spaniel)" title="Dash (spaniel)">Dash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Her lessons included French, German, Italian, and Latin,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> but she spoke only English at home.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Victoria&#39;s sketch of herself" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg/170px-Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg/255px-Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg/340px-Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="626" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_sketch_1835.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Self-portrait, 1835</div></div></div>
<p>In 1830, the Duchess of Kent and Conroy took Victoria across the centre of England to visit the <a href="/wiki/Malvern_Hills" title="Malvern Hills">Malvern Hills</a>, stopping at towns and great <a href="/wiki/Country_houses" class="mw-redirect" title="Country houses">country houses</a> along the way.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Similar journeys to other parts of England and Wales were taken in 1832, 1833, 1834 and 1835. To the King's annoyance, Victoria was enthusiastically welcomed in each of the stops.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> William compared the journeys to <a href="/wiki/Royal_progress" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal progress">royal progresses</a> and was concerned that they portrayed Victoria as his rival rather than his heir presumptive.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Victoria disliked the trips; the constant round of public appearances made her tired and ill, and there was little time for her to rest.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> She objected on the grounds of the King's disapproval, but her mother dismissed his complaints as motivated by jealousy and forced Victoria to continue the tours.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Ramsgate" title="Ramsgate">Ramsgate</a> in October 1835, Victoria contracted a severe fever, which Conroy initially dismissed as a childish pretence.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> While Victoria was ill, Conroy and the Duchess unsuccessfully badgered her to make Conroy her private secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> As a teenager, Victoria resisted persistent attempts by her mother and Conroy to appoint him to her staff.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Once queen, she banned him from her presence, but he remained in her mother's household.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1836, Victoria's maternal uncle <a href="/wiki/Leopold_I,_King_of_the_Belgians" class="mw-redirect" title="Leopold I, King of the Belgians">Leopold</a>, who had been King of the Belgians since 1831, hoped to marry her to <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Prince_Consort" title="Albert, Prince Consort">Prince Albert</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> the son of his brother <a href="/wiki/Ernest_I,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Ernest I, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Ernest I, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a>. Leopold arranged for Victoria's mother to invite her Coburg relatives to visit her in May 1836, with the purpose of introducing Victoria to Albert.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a>, however, disapproved of any match with the Coburgs, and instead favoured the suit of <a href="/wiki/Prince_Alexander_of_the_Netherlands" title="Prince Alexander of the Netherlands">Prince Alexander of the Netherlands</a>, second son of <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_the_Netherlands" title="William II of the Netherlands">the Prince of Orange</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Victoria was aware of the various matrimonial plans and critically appraised a parade of eligible princes.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> According to her diary, she enjoyed Albert's company from the beginning. After the visit she wrote, "[Albert] is extremely handsome; his hair is about the same colour as mine; his eyes are large and blue, and he has a beautiful nose and a very sweet mouth with fine teeth; but the charm of his countenance is his expression, which is most delightful."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Alexander, on the other hand, she described as "very plain".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victoria wrote to King Leopold, whom she considered her "best and kindest adviser",<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> to thank him "for the prospect of <i>great</i> happiness you have contributed to give me, in the person of dear Albert&#160;... He possesses every quality that could be desired to render me perfectly happy. He is so sensible, so kind, and so good, and so amiable too. He has besides the most pleasing and delightful exterior and appearance you can possibly see."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> However at 17, Victoria, though interested in Albert, was not yet ready to marry. The parties did not undertake a formal engagement, but assumed that the match would take place in due time.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reign">Early reign</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Coronation of Queen Victoria">Coronation of Queen Victoria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoriatothrone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of two men on their knees in front of Victoria" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Victoriatothrone.jpg/220px-Victoriatothrone.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Victoriatothrone.jpg/330px-Victoriatothrone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Victoriatothrone.jpg/440px-Victoriatothrone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="761" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoriatothrone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria receives the news of her accession from <a href="/wiki/Francis_Conyngham,_2nd_Marquess_Conyngham" title="Francis Conyngham, 2nd Marquess Conyngham">Lord Conyngham</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/William_Howley" title="William Howley">the Archbishop of Canterbury</a>. Engraving after painting by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Tanworth_Wells" title="Henry Tanworth Wells">Henry Tanworth Wells</a>, 1887</div></div></div>
<p>Victoria turned 18 on 24 May 1837, and a regency was avoided. Less than a month later, on 20 June 1837, William IV died at the age of 71, and Victoria became Queen of the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In her diary she wrote, "I was awoke at 6 o'clock by Mamma, who told me the <a href="/wiki/William_Howley" title="William Howley">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Conyngham,_2nd_Marquess_Conyngham" title="Francis Conyngham, 2nd Marquess Conyngham">Lord Conyngham</a> were here and wished to see me. I got out of bed and went into my sitting-room (only in my dressing gown) and <i>alone</i>, and saw them. Lord Conyngham then acquainted me that my poor Uncle, the King, was no more, and had expired at 12 minutes past 2 this morning, and consequently that <i>I</i> am <i>Queen</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Official documents prepared on the first day of her reign described her as Alexandrina Victoria, but the first name was withdrawn at her own wish and not used again.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1714, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> had shared a monarch with <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Hanover</a> in Germany, but under <a href="/wiki/Salic_law" title="Salic law">Salic law</a> women were excluded from the Hanoverian succession. While Victoria inherited all the British <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">Dominions</a>, her father's unpopular younger brother, the <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Augustus,_King_of_Hanover" title="Ernest Augustus, King of Hanover">Duke of Cumberland</a>, became <a href="/wiki/King_of_Hanover" title="King of Hanover">King of Hanover</a>. He was her <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a> while she was childless.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dronning_victoria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Dronning_victoria.jpg/220px-Dronning_victoria.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Dronning_victoria.jpg/330px-Dronning_victoria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Dronning_victoria.jpg/440px-Dronning_victoria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1212" data-file-height="1441" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dronning_victoria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coronation portrait by <a href="/wiki/George_Hayter" title="George Hayter">George Hayter</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the time of Victoria's accession, the government was led by the <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a> prime minister <a href="/wiki/Lord_Melbourne" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Melbourne">Lord Melbourne</a>. The Prime Minister at once became a powerful influence on the politically inexperienced Queen, who relied on him for advice.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Greville_(diarist)" title="Charles Greville (diarist)">Charles Greville</a> supposed that the widowed and childless Melbourne was "passionately fond of her as he might be of his daughter if he had one", and Victoria probably saw him as a father figure.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Coronation of Queen Victoria">Her coronation</a> took place on 28 June 1838 at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. Over 400,000 visitors came to London for the celebrations.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> She became the first sovereign to take up residence at <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and inherited the revenues of the duchies of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Lancaster" title="Duchy of Lancaster">Lancaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Cornwall" title="Duchy of Cornwall">Cornwall</a> as well as being granted a <a href="/wiki/Civil_list" title="Civil list">civil list</a> allowance of £385,000 per year. Financially prudent, she paid off her father's debts.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the start of her reign Victoria was popular,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> but her reputation suffered in an 1839 court intrigue when one of her mother's ladies-in-waiting, <a href="/wiki/Lady_Flora_Hastings" title="Lady Flora Hastings">Lady Flora Hastings</a>, developed an abdominal growth that was widely rumoured to be an out-of-wedlock pregnancy by Sir John Conroy.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Victoria believed the rumours.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> She hated Conroy, and despised "that odious Lady Flora",<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> because she had conspired with Conroy and the Duchess of Kent in the Kensington System.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> At first, Lady Flora refused to submit to an intimate medical examination, until in mid-February she eventually agreed, and was found to be a virgin.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Conroy, the Hastings family, and the opposition <a href="/wiki/Tories" class="mw-redirect" title="Tories">Tories</a> organised a press campaign implicating the Queen in the spreading of false rumours about Lady Flora.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> When Lady Flora died in July, the post-mortem revealed a large tumour on her liver that had distended her abdomen.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> At public appearances, Victoria was hissed and jeered as "Mrs. Melbourne".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1839, Melbourne resigned after <a href="/wiki/Radicals_(UK)" title="Radicals (UK)">Radicals</a> and Tories (both of whom Victoria detested) voted against a bill to suspend the constitution of <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>. The bill removed political power from plantation owners who were resisting measures associated with the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Abolitionism in the United Kingdom">abolition of slavery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The Queen commissioned a Tory, Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a>, to form a new ministry. At the time, it was customary for the prime minister to appoint members of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Households_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal Households of the United Kingdom">Royal Household</a>, who were usually his political allies and their spouses. Many of the Queen's <a href="/wiki/Ladies_of_the_bedchamber" class="mw-redirect" title="Ladies of the bedchamber">ladies of the bedchamber</a> were wives of Whigs, and Peel expected to replace them with wives of Tories. In what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Bedchamber_crisis" title="Bedchamber crisis">bedchamber crisis</a>, Victoria, advised by Melbourne, objected to their removal. Peel refused to govern under the restrictions imposed by the Queen, and consequently resigned his commission, allowing Melbourne to return to office.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wedding_of_Queen_Victoria_and_Prince_Albert_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Wedding of Queen Victoria and Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Wedding of Queen Victoria and Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wedding_dress_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Wedding dress of Queen Victoria">Wedding dress of Queen Victoria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Marriage01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of a lavish wedding attended by richly dressed people in a magnificent room" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Victoria_Marriage01.jpg/310px-Victoria_Marriage01.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Victoria_Marriage01.jpg/465px-Victoria_Marriage01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Victoria_Marriage01.jpg/620px-Victoria_Marriage01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1061" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Marriage01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marriage of Victoria and Albert, painted by <a href="/wiki/George_Hayter" title="George Hayter">George Hayter</a></div></div></div>
<p>Though Victoria was now queen, as an unmarried young woman she was required by social convention to live with her mother, despite their differences over the Kensington System and her mother's continued reliance on Conroy.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Her mother was consigned to a remote apartment in Buckingham Palace, and Victoria often refused to see her.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> When Victoria complained to Melbourne that her mother's close proximity promised "torment for many years", Melbourne sympathised but said it could be avoided by marriage, which Victoria called a "schocking &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93; alternative".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Victoria showed interest in Albert's education for the future role he would have to play as her husband, but she resisted attempts to rush her into wedlock.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victoria continued to praise Albert following his second visit in October 1839. Albert and Victoria felt mutual affection and the Queen proposed to him on 15 October 1839, just five days after he had arrived at Windsor.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> They were married on 10 February 1840, in the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a> of <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Palace" title="St James&#39;s Palace">St James's Palace</a>, London. Victoria was love-struck. She spent the evening after their wedding lying down with a headache, but wrote ecstatically in her diary:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I NEVER, NEVER spent such an evening!!! MY DEAREST DEAREST DEAR Albert&#160;... his excessive love &amp; affection gave me feelings of heavenly love &amp; happiness I never could have <i>hoped</i> to have felt before! He clasped me in his arms, &amp; we kissed each other again &amp; again! His beauty, his sweetness &amp; gentleness – really how can I ever be thankful enough to have such a <i>Husband</i>!&#160;... to be called by names of tenderness, I have never yet heard used to me before – was bliss beyond belief! Oh! This was the happiest day of my life!<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Albert became an important political adviser as well as the Queen's companion, replacing Melbourne as the dominant influential figure in the first half of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Victoria's mother was evicted from the palace, to Ingestre House in <a href="/wiki/Belgrave_Square" title="Belgrave Square">Belgrave Square</a>. After the death of Victoria's aunt, <a href="/wiki/Princess_Augusta_Sophia_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Princess Augusta Sophia of the United Kingdom">Princess Augusta</a>, in 1840, Victoria's mother was given both <a href="/wiki/Clarence_House" title="Clarence House">Clarence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frogmore_House" title="Frogmore House">Frogmore Houses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Through Albert's mediation, relations between mother and daughter slowly improved.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_Oxford_shoots_at_H._M._the_Queen,_1840.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edward_Oxford_shoots_at_H._M._the_Queen%2C_1840.jpg/310px-Edward_Oxford_shoots_at_H._M._the_Queen%2C_1840.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Edward_Oxford_shoots_at_H._M._the_Queen%2C_1840.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="392" data-file-height="309" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_Oxford_shoots_at_H._M._the_Queen,_1840.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary lithograph of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Oxford" title="Edward Oxford">Edward Oxford</a>'s attempt to assassinate Victoria, 1840</div></div></div>
<p>During Victoria's first pregnancy in 1840, in the first few months of the marriage, 18-year-old <a href="/wiki/Edward_Oxford" title="Edward Oxford">Edward Oxford</a> attempted to assassinate her while she was riding in a carriage with Prince Albert on her way to visit her mother. Oxford fired twice, but either both bullets missed or, as he later claimed, the guns had no shot.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> He was tried for <a href="/wiki/High_treason" class="mw-redirect" title="High treason">high treason</a>, found <a href="/wiki/Not_guilty_by_reason_of_insanity" class="mw-redirect" title="Not guilty by reason of insanity">not guilty by reason of insanity</a>, committed to an insane asylum indefinitely, and later sent to live in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In the immediate aftermath of the attack, Victoria's popularity soared, mitigating residual discontent over the Hastings affair and the bedchamber crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Her daughter, also named <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Princess_Royal" title="Victoria, Princess Royal">Victoria</a>, was born on 21 November 1840. The Queen hated being pregnant,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> viewed breast-feeding with disgust,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and thought newborn babies were ugly.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, over the following seventeen years, she and Albert had a further eight children: <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">Albert Edward</a> (b. 1841), <a href="/wiki/Princess_Alice_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Princess Alice of the United Kingdom">Alice</a> (b. 1843), <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Alfred, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Alfred</a> (b. 1844), <a href="/wiki/Princess_Helena_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Princess Helena of the United Kingdom">Helena</a> (b. 1846), <a href="/wiki/Princess_Louise,_Duchess_of_Argyll" title="Princess Louise, Duchess of Argyll">Louise</a> (b. 1848), <a href="/wiki/Prince_Arthur,_Duke_of_Connaught_and_Strathearn" title="Prince Arthur, Duke of Connaught and Strathearn">Arthur</a> (b. 1850), <a href="/wiki/Prince_Leopold,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Prince Leopold, Duke of Albany">Leopold</a> (b. 1853) and <a href="/wiki/Princess_Beatrice_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Princess Beatrice of the United Kingdom">Beatrice</a> (b. 1857).
</p><p>Victoria's household was largely run by her childhood governess, Baroness <a href="/wiki/Louise_Lehzen" title="Louise Lehzen">Louise Lehzen</a> from <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Hanover</a>. Lehzen had been a formative influence on Victoria<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and had supported her against the Kensington System.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Albert, however, thought that Lehzen was incompetent and that her mismanagement threatened his daughter's health. After a furious row between Victoria and Albert over the issue, Lehzen was pensioned off in 1842, and Victoria's close relationship with her ended.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="1842.E2.80.931860"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1842–1860">1842–1860</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg/170px-Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg/255px-Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg/340px-Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1231" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Winterhalter_-_Queen_Victoria_1843.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Xaver_Winterhalter" title="Franz Xaver Winterhalter">Franz Xaver Winterhalter</a>, 1843</div></div></div>
<p>On 29 May 1842, Victoria was riding in a carriage along <a href="/wiki/The_Mall,_London" title="The Mall, London">The Mall, London</a>, when John Francis aimed a pistol at her, but the gun did not fire. The assailant escaped; however the following day, Victoria drove the same route, though faster and with a greater escort, in a deliberate attempt to provoke Francis to take a second aim and catch him in the act. As expected, Francis shot at her, but he was seized by plainclothes policemen, and convicted of high treason. On 3 July, two days after Francis's death sentence was commuted to <a href="/wiki/Transportation_for_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Transportation for life">transportation for life</a>, John William Bean also tried to fire a pistol at the Queen, but it was loaded only with paper and tobacco and had too little charge.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Edward Oxford felt that the attempts were encouraged by his acquittal in 1840. Bean was sentenced to 18 months in jail.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In a similar attack in 1849, unemployed Irishman William Hamilton fired a powder-filled pistol at Victoria's carriage as it passed along <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Hill,_London" title="Constitution Hill, London">Constitution Hill, London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In 1850, the Queen did sustain injury when she was assaulted by a possibly insane ex-army officer, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Pate" title="Robert Pate">Robert Pate</a>. As Victoria was riding in a carriage, Pate struck her with his cane, crushing her bonnet and bruising her forehead. Both Hamilton and Pate were sentenced to seven years' transportation.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Melbourne's support in the House of Commons weakened through the early years of Victoria's reign, and in the <a href="/wiki/1841_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1841 United Kingdom general election">1841 general election</a> the Whigs were defeated. Peel became prime minister, and the ladies of the bedchamber most associated with the Whigs were replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" class="image"><img alt="Victoria cuddling a child next to her" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/170px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/255px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/340px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png 2x" data-file-width="1153" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earliest known photograph of Victoria, here with her eldest daughter, c. 1845<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 1845, Ireland was hit by a <a href="/wiki/Potato_blight" class="mw-redirect" title="Potato blight">potato blight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In the next four years, over a million Irish people died and another million emigrated in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In Ireland, Victoria was labelled "The Famine Queen".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In January 1847 she personally donated £2,000 (equivalent to between £178,000 and £6.5&#160;million in 2016<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>) to the <a href="/wiki/British_Relief_Association" title="British Relief Association">British Relief Association</a>, more than any other individual famine relief donor,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and also supported the <a href="/wiki/Maynooth_Grant" title="Maynooth Grant">Maynooth Grant</a> to a Roman Catholic seminary in Ireland, despite Protestant opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The story that she donated only £5 in aid to the Irish, and on the same day gave the same amount to <a href="/wiki/Battersea_Dogs_Home" class="mw-redirect" title="Battersea Dogs Home">Battersea Dogs Home</a>, was a myth generated towards the end of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1846, Peel's ministry faced a crisis involving the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a>. Many Tories—by then known also as <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a>—were opposed to the repeal, but Peel, some Tories (the "Peelites"), most Whigs and Victoria supported it. Peel resigned in 1846, after the repeal narrowly passed, and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord John Russell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td colspan="2">See <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Queen_Victoria" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Prime Ministers of Queen Victoria">List of Prime Ministers of Queen Victoria</a><br />for details of her British and Imperial premiers
