<p>Internationally, Victoria took a keen interest in the improvement of relations between France and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> She made and hosted several visits between the British royal family and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Orleans" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Orleans">House of Orleans</a>, who were related by marriage through the Coburgs. In 1843 and 1845, she and Albert stayed with King <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis Philippe I</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_d%27Eu" title="Château d&#39;Eu">château d'Eu</a> in Normandy; she was the first British or English monarch to visit a French monarch since the meeting of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I of France</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Field_of_the_Cloth_of_Gold" title="Field of the Cloth of Gold">Field of the Cloth of Gold</a> in 1520.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> When Louis Philippe made a reciprocal trip in 1844, he became the first French king to visit a British sovereign.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Louis Philippe was deposed in the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolutions of 1848</a>, and fled to exile in England.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> At the height of a revolutionary scare in the United Kingdom in April 1848, Victoria and her family left London for the greater safety of <a href="/wiki/Osborne_House" title="Osborne House">Osborne House</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> a private estate on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a> that they had purchased in 1845 and redeveloped.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Demonstrations by <a href="/wiki/Chartists" class="mw-redirect" title="Chartists">Chartists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish nationalists">Irish nationalists</a> failed to attract widespread support, and the scare died down without any major disturbances.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Victoria's first visit to Ireland in 1849 was a public relations success, but it had no lasting impact or effect on the growth of Irish nationalism.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Russell's ministry, though Whig, was not favoured by the Queen.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> She found particularly offensive the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Palmerston" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, who often acted without consulting the Cabinet, the Prime Minister, or the Queen.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Victoria complained to Russell that Palmerston sent official dispatches to foreign leaders without her knowledge, but Palmerston was retained in office and continued to act on his own initiative, despite her repeated remonstrances. It was only in 1851 that Palmerston was removed after he announced the British government's approval of President <a href="/wiki/Louis-Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis-Napoleon Bonaparte">Louis-Napoleon Bonaparte</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Coup" class="mw-redirect" title="Coup">coup</a> in France without consulting the Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The following year, President Bonaparte was declared Emperor Napoleon III, by which time Russell's administration had been replaced by a short-lived minority government led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith-Stanley,_14th_Earl_of_Derby" title="Edward Smith-Stanley, 14th Earl of Derby">Lord Derby</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of a seated Victoria, dressed in black, holding an infant with her children and Prince Albert standing around her" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG/400px-Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG" decoding="async" width="400" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG/600px-Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG/800px-Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2207" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_Prince_Albert_and_their_nine_children.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Albert, Victoria and their nine children, 1857. Left to right: Alice, Arthur, Prince Albert, Albert Edward, Leopold, Louise, Queen Victoria with Beatrice, Alfred, Victoria and Helena</div></div></div>
<p>In 1853, Victoria gave birth to her eighth child, Leopold, with the aid of the new anaesthetic, <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>. She was so impressed by the relief it gave from the pain of childbirth that she used it again in 1857 at the birth of her ninth and final child, Beatrice, despite opposition from members of the clergy, who considered it against biblical teaching, and members of the medical profession, who thought it dangerous.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Victoria may have suffered from <a href="/wiki/Postnatal_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Postnatal depression">postnatal depression</a> after many of her pregnancies.<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Letters from Albert to Victoria intermittently complain of her loss of self-control. For example, about a month after Leopold's birth Albert complained in a letter to Victoria about her "continuance of hysterics" over a "miserable trifle".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 1855, the government of <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton-Gordon,_4th_Earl_of_Aberdeen" title="George Hamilton-Gordon, 4th Earl of Aberdeen">Lord Aberdeen</a>, who had replaced Derby, fell amidst recriminations over the poor management of British troops in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>. Victoria approached both Derby and Russell to form a ministry, but neither had sufficient support, and Victoria was forced to appoint Palmerston as prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Napoleon III, who had been Britain's closest ally since the Crimean War,<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> visited London in April 1855, and from 17 to 28 August the same year Victoria and Albert returned the visit.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Napoleon III met the couple at <a href="/wiki/Boulogne-sur-Mer" title="Boulogne-sur-Mer">Boulogne</a> and accompanied them to Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> They visited the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1855)" title="Exposition Universelle (1855)">Exposition Universelle</a> (a successor to Albert's 1851 brainchild the <a href="/wiki/Great_Exhibition" title="Great Exhibition">Great Exhibition</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon I</a>'s tomb at <a href="/wiki/Les_Invalides" title="Les Invalides">Les Invalides</a> (to which his remains had only been <a href="/wiki/Retour_des_cendres" title="Retour des cendres">returned</a> in 1840), and were guests of honour at a 1,200-guest ball at the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg/170px-Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg/255px-Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg/340px-Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1463" data-file-height="2250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_-_Winterhalter_1859.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait by Winterhalter, 1859</div></div></div>
<p>On 14 January 1858, an Italian refugee from Britain called <a href="/wiki/Felice_Orsini" title="Felice Orsini">Felice Orsini</a> attempted to assassinate Napoleon III with a bomb made in England.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The ensuing diplomatic crisis destabilised the government, and Palmerston resigned. Derby was reinstated as prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Victoria and Albert attended the opening of a new basin at the French military port of <a href="/wiki/Cherbourg" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherbourg">Cherbourg</a> on 5 August 1858, in an attempt by Napoleon III to reassure Britain that his military preparations were directed elsewhere. On her return Victoria wrote to Derby reprimanding him for the poor state of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> in comparison to the <a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French one</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Derby's ministry did not last long, and in June 1859 Victoria recalled Palmerston to office.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eleven days after Orsini's assassination attempt in France, Victoria's eldest daughter married <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_German_Emperor" title="Frederick III, German Emperor">Prince Frederick William of Prussia</a> in London. They had been betrothed since September 1855, when Princess Victoria was 14 years old; the marriage was delayed by the Queen and her husband Albert until the bride was 17.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The Queen and Albert hoped that their daughter and son-in-law would be a liberalising influence in the enlarging <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> state.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The Queen felt "sick at heart" to see her daughter leave England for Germany; "It really makes me shudder", she wrote to Princess Victoria in one of her frequent letters, "when I look round to all your sweet, happy, unconscious sisters, and think I must give them up too – one by one."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Almost exactly a year later, the Princess gave birth to the Queen's first grandchild, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Wilhelm</a>, who would become the last German Emperor.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Widowhood">Widowhood</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall,_1860.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall%2C_1860.png/170px-Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall%2C_1860.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall%2C_1860.png/255px-Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall%2C_1860.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall%2C_1860.png/340px-Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall%2C_1860.png 2x" data-file-width="1147" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_JJE_Mayall,_1860.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria photographed by <a href="/wiki/John_Jabez_Edwin_Mayall" title="John Jabez Edwin Mayall">J. J. E. Mayall</a>, 1860</div></div></div>
<p>In March 1861, Victoria's mother died, with Victoria at her side. Through reading her mother's papers, Victoria discovered that her mother had loved her deeply;<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> she was heart-broken, and blamed Conroy and Lehzen for "wickedly" estranging her from her mother.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> To relieve his wife during her intense and deep grief,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Albert took on most of her duties, despite being ill himself with chronic stomach trouble.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In August, Victoria and Albert visited their son, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Edward,_Prince_of_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Edward, Prince of Wales">Albert Edward, Prince of Wales</a>, who was attending army manoeuvres near Dublin, and spent a few days holidaying in <a href="/wiki/Killarney" title="Killarney">Killarney</a>. In November, Albert was made aware of gossip that his son had slept with an actress in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Appalled, he travelled to Cambridge, where his son was studying, to confront him.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> By the beginning of December, Albert was very unwell.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> He was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Jenner,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir William Jenner, 1st Baronet">William Jenner</a>, and died on 14 December 1861. Victoria was devastated.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> She blamed her husband's death on worry over the Prince of Wales's philandering. He had been "killed by that dreadful business", she said.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> She entered a state of <a href="/wiki/Mourning" title="Mourning">mourning</a> and wore black for the remainder of her life. She avoided public appearances, and rarely set foot in London in the following years.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Her seclusion earned her the nickname "widow of Windsor".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Her weight increased through comfort eating, which further reinforced her aversion to public appearances.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victoria's self-imposed isolation from the public diminished the popularity of the monarchy, and encouraged the growth of the republican movement.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> She did undertake her official government duties, yet chose to remain secluded in her royal residences—<a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osborne_House" title="Osborne House">Osborne House</a>, and the private estate in Scotland that she and Albert had acquired in 1847, <a href="/wiki/Balmoral_Castle" title="Balmoral Castle">Balmoral Castle</a>. In March 1864 a protester stuck a notice on the railings of <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> that announced "these commanding premises to be let or sold in consequence of the late occupant's declining business".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Her uncle Leopold wrote to her advising her to appear in public. She agreed to visit the gardens of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horticultural_Society" title="Royal Horticultural Society">Royal Horticultural Society</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kensington" title="Kensington">Kensington</a> and take a drive through London in an open carriage.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria,_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_(1863).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Queen_Victoria%2C_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_%281863%29.jpg/170px-Queen_Victoria%2C_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_%281863%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Queen_Victoria%2C_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_%281863%29.jpg/255px-Queen_Victoria%2C_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_%281863%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Queen_Victoria%2C_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_%281863%29.jpg/340px-Queen_Victoria%2C_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_%281863%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="762" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria,_photographed_by_George_Washington_Wilson_(1863).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria and John Brown at Balmoral, 1863. Photograph by <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Wilson" title="George Washington Wilson">G. W. Wilson</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Through the 1860s, Victoria relied increasingly on a manservant from Scotland, <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(servant)" title="John Brown (servant)">John Brown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Slanderous rumours of a romantic connection and even a secret marriage appeared in print, and the Queen was referred to as "Mrs. Brown".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The story of their relationship was the subject of the 1997 movie <i><a href="/wiki/Mrs._Brown_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mrs. Brown (film)">Mrs. Brown</a></i>. A painting by Sir <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Henry_Landseer" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Henry Landseer">Edwin Henry Landseer</a> depicting the Queen with Brown was exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a>, and Victoria published a book, <i>Leaves from the Journal of Our Life in the Highlands</i>, which featured Brown prominently and in which the Queen praised him highly.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Palmerston died in 1865, and after a brief ministry led by Russell, Derby returned to power. In 1866, Victoria attended the <a href="/wiki/State_Opening_of_Parliament" title="State Opening of Parliament">State Opening of Parliament</a> for the first time since Albert's death.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The following year she supported the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1867" title="Reform Act 1867">Reform Act 1867</a> which doubled the electorate by extending the franchise to many urban working men,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> though she was not in favour of votes for women.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Derby resigned in 1868, to be replaced by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, who charmed Victoria. "Everyone likes flattery," he said, "and when you come to royalty you should lay it on with a trowel."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> With the phrase "we authors, Ma'am", he complimented her.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Disraeli's ministry only lasted a matter of months, and at the end of the year his Liberal rival, <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>, was appointed prime minister. Victoria found Gladstone's demeanour far less appealing; he spoke to her, she is thought to have complained, as though she were "a public meeting rather than a woman".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1870 republican sentiment in Britain, fed by the Queen's seclusion, was boosted after the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Third_French_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Third French Republic">Third French Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> A republican rally in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> demanded Victoria's removal, and Radical MPs spoke against her.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In August and September 1871, she was seriously ill with an <a href="/wiki/Abscess" title="Abscess">abscess</a> in her arm, which <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister" title="Joseph Lister">Joseph Lister</a> successfully lanced and treated with his new antiseptic <a href="/wiki/Carbolic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbolic acid">carbolic acid</a> spray.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In late November 1871, at the height of the republican movement, the Prince of Wales contracted typhoid fever, the disease that was believed to have killed his father, and Victoria was fearful her son would die.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> As the tenth anniversary of her husband's death approached, her son's condition grew no better, and Victoria's distress continued.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> To general rejoicing, he recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Mother and son attended a public parade through London and a grand service of thanksgiving in <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> on 27 February 1872, and republican feeling subsided.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the last day of February 1872, two days after the thanksgiving service, 17-year-old Arthur O'Connor, a great-nephew of Irish MP <a href="/wiki/Feargus_O%27Connor" title="Feargus O&#39;Connor">Feargus O'Connor</a>, waved an unloaded pistol at Victoria's open carriage just after she had arrived at Buckingham Palace. Brown, who was attending the Queen, grabbed him and O'Connor was later sentenced to 12 months' imprisonment,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Birching" title="Birching">birching</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the incident, Victoria's popularity recovered further.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Empress_of_India">Empress of India</span></h2>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/38px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="38" height="40" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/57px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/76px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist"><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Queen_Victoria%27s_Proclamation" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Queen Victoria&#39;s Proclamation">Queen Victoria's Proclamation</a></b></div></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a>, which had ruled much of India, was dissolved, and Britain's possessions and protectorates on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> were formally incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. The Queen had a relatively balanced view of the conflict, and condemned atrocities on both sides.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> She wrote of "her feelings of horror and regret at the result of this bloody civil war",<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and insisted, urged on by Albert, that an official proclamation announcing the transfer of power from the company to the state "should breathe feelings of generosity, benevolence and religious toleration".<sup id="cite_ref-hws_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hws-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> At her behest, a reference threatening the "undermining of native religions and customs" was replaced by a passage guaranteeing religious freedom.<sup id="cite_ref-hws_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hws-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg/170px-Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg/255px-Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg/340px-Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg 2x" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_by_Julia_Abercromby.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria admired <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Angeli" title="Heinrich von Angeli">Heinrich von Angeli</a>'s 1875 portrait of her for its "honesty, total want of flattery, and appreciation of character".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/1874_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1874 United Kingdom general election">1874 general election</a>, Disraeli was returned to power. He passed the <a href="/wiki/Public_Worship_Regulation_Act_1874" title="Public Worship Regulation Act 1874">Public Worship Regulation Act 1874</a>, which removed Catholic rituals from the Anglican liturgy and which Victoria strongly supported.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> She preferred short, simple services, and personally considered herself more aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">presbyterian</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopal</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Disraeli also pushed the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Titles_Act_1876" title="Royal Titles Act 1876">Royal Titles Act 1876</a> through Parliament, so that Victoria took the title "Empress of India" from 1 May 1876.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The new title was proclaimed at the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Durbar" title="Delhi Durbar">Delhi Durbar</a> of 1 January 1877.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 December 1878, the anniversary of Albert's death, Victoria's second daughter Alice, who had married <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV,_Grand_Duke_of_Hesse" title="Louis IV, Grand Duke of Hesse">Louis of Hesse</a>, died of <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Darmstadt" title="Darmstadt">Darmstadt</a>. Victoria noted the coincidence of the dates as "almost incredible and most mysterious".<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In May 1879, she became a great-grandmother (on the birth of <a href="/wiki/Princess_Feodora_of_Saxe-Meiningen" title="Princess Feodora of Saxe-Meiningen">Princess Feodora of Saxe-Meiningen</a>) and passed her "poor old 60th birthday". She felt "aged" by "the loss of my beloved child".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between April 1877 and February 1878, she threatened five times to abdicate while pressuring Disraeli to act against Russia during the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%931878)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877–1878)">Russo-Turkish War</a>, but her threats had no impact on the events or their conclusion with the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Disraeli's expansionist foreign policy, which Victoria endorsed, led to conflicts such as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">Anglo-Zulu War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Afghan_War" title="Second Anglo-Afghan War">Second Anglo-Afghan War</a>. "If <i>we</i> are to <i>maintain</i> our position as a <i>first-rate</i> Power", she wrote, "we must&#160;... be <i>Prepared</i> for <i>attacks</i> and <i>wars</i>, <i>somewhere</i> or <i>other</i>, CONTINUALLY."<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Victoria saw the expansion of the British Empire as civilising and benign, protecting native peoples from more aggressive powers or cruel rulers: "It is not in our custom to annexe countries", she said, "unless we are obliged &amp; forced to do so."<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> To Victoria's dismay, Disraeli lost the <a href="/wiki/1880_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1880 United Kingdom general election">1880 general election</a>, and Gladstone returned as prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> When Disraeli died the following year, she was blinded by "fast falling tears",<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> and erected a memorial tablet "placed by his grateful Sovereign and Friend, Victoria R.I."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_farthing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Victoria_farthing.jpg/170px-Victoria_farthing.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Victoria_farthing.jpg/255px-Victoria_farthing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Victoria_farthing.jpg/340px-Victoria_farthing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="397" data-file-height="396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_farthing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victorian <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">farthing</a>, 1884</div></div></div>
<p>On 2 March 1882, <a href="/wiki/Roderick_Maclean" title="Roderick Maclean">Roderick Maclean</a>, a disgruntled poet apparently offended by Victoria's refusal to accept one of his poems,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> shot at the Queen as her carriage left <a href="/wiki/Windsor_%26_Eton_Central_railway_station" title="Windsor &amp; Eton Central railway station">Windsor railway station</a>. Two schoolboys from <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton College</a> struck him with their umbrellas, until he was hustled away by a policeman.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Victoria was outraged when he was found not guilty by reason of insanity,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> but was so pleased by the many expressions of loyalty after the attack that she said it was "worth being shot at—to see how much one is loved".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 17 March 1883, Victoria fell down some stairs at Windsor, which left her lame until July; she never fully recovered and was plagued with rheumatism thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Brown died 10 days after her accident, and to the consternation of her private secretary, Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ponsonby" title="Henry Ponsonby">Henry Ponsonby</a>, Victoria began work on a eulogistic biography of Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Ponsonby and <a href="/wiki/Randall_Davidson" title="Randall Davidson">Randall Davidson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dean_of_Windsor" title="Dean of Windsor">Dean of Windsor</a>, who had both seen early drafts, advised Victoria against publication, on the grounds that it would stoke the rumours of a love affair.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The manuscript was destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> In early 1884, Victoria did publish <i>More Leaves from a Journal of a Life in the Highlands</i>, a sequel to her earlier book, which she dedicated to her "devoted personal attendant and faithful friend John Brown".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> On the day after the first anniversary of Brown's death, Victoria was informed by telegram that her youngest son, Leopold, had died in <a href="/wiki/Cannes" title="Cannes">Cannes</a>. He was "the dearest of my dear sons", she lamented.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The following month, Victoria's youngest child, Beatrice, met and fell in love with <a href="/wiki/Prince_Henry_of_Battenberg" title="Prince Henry of Battenberg">Prince Henry of Battenberg</a> at the wedding of Victoria's granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Princess_Victoria_of_Hesse_and_by_Rhine" title="Princess Victoria of Hesse and by Rhine">Princess Victoria of Hesse and by Rhine</a> to Henry's brother <a href="/wiki/Prince_Louis_of_Battenberg" title="Prince Louis of Battenberg">Prince Louis of Battenberg</a>. Beatrice and Henry planned to marry, but Victoria opposed the match at first, wishing to keep Beatrice at home to act as her companion. After a year, she was won around to the marriage by their promise to remain living with and attending her.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_1898.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/British_Empire_in_1898.png/290px-British_Empire_in_1898.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/British_Empire_in_1898.png/435px-British_Empire_in_1898.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/British_Empire_in_1898.png/580px-British_Empire_in_1898.png 2x" data-file-width="1434" data-file-height="624" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_1898.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extent of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> in 1898</div></div></div>
<p>Victoria was pleased when Gladstone resigned in 1885 after his budget was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> She thought his government was "the worst I have ever had", and blamed him for the death of <a href="/wiki/General_Gordon" class="mw-redirect" title="General Gordon">General Gordon</a> at <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Gladstone was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Lord_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>. Salisbury's government only lasted a few months, however, and Victoria was forced to recall Gladstone, whom she referred to as a "half crazy &amp; really in many ways ridiculous old man".<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Gladstone attempted to pass <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Bill_1886" title="Government of Ireland Bill 1886">a bill granting Ireland home rule</a>, but to Victoria's glee it was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1886_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1886 United Kingdom general election">the ensuing election</a>, Gladstone's party lost to Salisbury's and the government switched hands again.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_Jubilee">Golden Jubilee</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Golden Jubilee of Queen Victoria">Golden Jubilee of Queen Victoria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The Munshi stands over Victoria as she works at a desk" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg/170px-Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg/255px-Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg/340px-Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg 2x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_and_Abdul_Karim.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria and the <a href="/wiki/Munshi" title="Munshi">Munshi</a> Abdul Karim</div></div></div>
<p>In 1887, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> celebrated the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Jubilee_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Golden Jubilee of Queen Victoria">Golden Jubilee of Queen Victoria</a>. She marked the fiftieth anniversary of her accession on 20 June with a banquet to which 50 kings and princes were invited. The following day, she participated in a procession and attended a thanksgiving service in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> By this time, Victoria was once again extremely popular.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Two days later on 23 June,<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> she engaged two Indian Muslims as waiters, one of whom was <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Karim_(the_Munshi)" title="Abdul Karim (the Munshi)">Abdul Karim</a>. He was soon promoted to "<a href="/wiki/Munshi" title="Munshi">Munshi</a>": teaching her <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a>) and acting as a clerk.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Her family and retainers were appalled, and accused Abdul Karim of spying for the Muslim Patriotic League, and biasing the Queen against the Hindus.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Equerry" title="Equerry">Equerry</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Ponsonby,_1st_Baron_Sysonby" title="Frederick Ponsonby, 1st Baron Sysonby">Frederick Ponsonby</a> (the son of Sir Henry) discovered that the Munshi had lied about his parentage, and reported to <a href="/wiki/Victor_Bruce,_9th_Earl_of_Elgin" title="Victor Bruce, 9th Earl of Elgin">Lord Elgin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viceroy_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Viceroy of India">Viceroy of India</a>, "the Munshi occupies very much the same position as John Brown used to do."<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Victoria dismissed their complaints as racial prejudice.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Abdul Karim remained in her service until he returned to India with a pension, on her death.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victoria's eldest daughter became Empress consort of Germany in 1888, but she was widowed within the year, and Victoria's eldest grandchild became German Emperor as Wilhelm II. Under Wilhelm, Victoria and Albert's hopes of a liberal Germany were not fulfilled. He believed in <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocracy</a>. Victoria thought he had "little heart or <i>Zartgefühl</i> [tact] – and&#160;... his conscience &amp; intelligence have been completely wharped &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93;".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gladstone returned to power after the <a href="/wiki/1892_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1892 United Kingdom general election">1892 general election</a>; he was 82 years old. Victoria objected when Gladstone proposed appointing the Radical MP <a href="/wiki/Henry_Labouch%C3%A8re" title="Henry Labouchère">Henry Labouchère</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a>, so Gladstone agreed not to appoint him.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In 1894, Gladstone retired and, without consulting the outgoing prime minister, Victoria appointed <a href="/wiki/Lord_Rosebery" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Rosebery">Lord Rosebery</a> as prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> His government was weak, and the following year Lord Salisbury replaced him. Salisbury remained prime minister for the remainder of Victoria's reign.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diamond_Jubilee">Diamond Jubilee</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Seated Victoria in embroidered and lace dress" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg/170px-Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg/255px-Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg/340px-Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="862" data-file-height="1138" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_60._crownjubilee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria in her official <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond Jubilee">Diamond Jubilee</a> photograph by <a href="/wiki/W._%26_D._Downey" title="W. &amp; D. Downey">W. &amp; D. Downey</a></div></div></div>
<p>On 23 September 1896, Victoria surpassed her grandfather George III as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_in_Britain_by_length_of_reign" title="List of monarchs in Britain by length of reign">longest-reigning monarch in British history</a>. The Queen requested that any special celebrations be delayed until 1897, to coincide with her <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond Jubilee">Diamond Jubilee</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> which was made a festival of the British Empire at the suggestion of the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Colonial Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The prime ministers of all the <a href="/wiki/Self-governing" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-governing">self-governing</a> Dominions were invited to London for the festivities.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> One reason for including the prime ministers of the Dominions and excluding foreign heads of state was to avoid having to invite Victoria's grandson, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Wilhelm II of Germany</a>, who, it was feared, might cause trouble at the event.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Queen's Diamond Jubilee procession on 22 June 1897 followed a route six miles long through London and included troops from all over the empire. The procession paused for an open-air service of thanksgiving held outside <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a>, throughout which Victoria sat in her open carriage, to avoid her having to climb the steps to enter the building. The celebration was marked by vast crowds of spectators and great outpourings of affection for the 78-year-old Queen.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;727184&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Queen Victoria In Dublin (Rare archive footage from 1900).webm" style="width:170px;height:96px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Queen_Victoria_In_Dublin_%28Rare_archive_footage_from_1900%29.webm/170px--Queen_Victoria_In_Dublin_%28Rare_archive_footage_from_1900%29.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Queen_Victoria_In_Dublin_%28Rare_archive_footage_from_1900%29.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_In_Dublin_(Rare_archive_footage_from_1900).webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen Victoria in Dublin, 1900</div></div></div>
<p>Victoria visited mainland Europe regularly for holidays. In 1889, during a stay in <a href="/wiki/Biarritz" title="Biarritz">Biarritz</a>, she became the first reigning monarch from Britain to set foot in Spain when she crossed the border for a brief visit.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> By April 1900, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Boer War</a> was so unpopular in mainland Europe that her annual trip to France seemed inadvisable. Instead, the Queen went to Ireland for the first time since 1861, in part to acknowledge the contribution of Irish regiments to the South African war.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession">Death and succession</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg/170px-Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg/255px-Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg/340px-Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3092" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_after_Heinrich_von_Angeli.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen Victoria aged 80, 1899</div></div></div>
<p>In July 1900, Victoria's second son Alfred ("Affie") died. "Oh, God! My poor darling Affie gone too", she wrote in her journal. "It is a horrible year, nothing but sadness &amp; horrors of one kind &amp; another."<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following a custom she maintained throughout her widowhood, Victoria spent the Christmas of 1900 at <a href="/wiki/Osborne_House" title="Osborne House">Osborne House</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>. Rheumatism in her legs had rendered her lame, and her eyesight was clouded by cataracts.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Through early January, she felt "weak and unwell",<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> and by mid-January she was "drowsy&#160;... dazed, [and] confused".<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> She died on Tuesday 22 January 1901, at half past six in the evening, at the age of 81.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Her son and successor, <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_VII" class="mw-redirect" title="King Edward VII">King Edward VII</a>, and her eldest grandson, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wilhelm_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Wilhelm II">Emperor Wilhelm II</a>, were at her deathbed.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Her favourite <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria%27s_pets" title="Queen Victoria&#39;s pets">pet</a> <a href="/wiki/Pomeranian_(dog)" title="Pomeranian (dog)">Pomeranian</a>, Turi, was laid upon her deathbed as a last request.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2,_1901.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2%2C_1901.jpg/170px-Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2%2C_1901.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2%2C_1901.jpg/255px-Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2%2C_1901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2%2C_1901.jpg/340px-Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2%2C_1901.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Proclamation_-_Day_of_mourning_in_Toronto_for_Queen_Victoria_February_2,_1901.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster proclaiming a day of mourning in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> on the day of <a href="/wiki/Funeral_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Funeral of Queen Victoria">Victoria's funeral</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1897, Victoria had written instructions for <a href="/wiki/Funeral_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Funeral of Queen Victoria">her funeral</a>, which was to be military as befitting a soldier's daughter and the head of the army,<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and white instead of black.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> On 25 January, Edward, Wilhelm and her third son, the Duke of Connaught, helped lift her body into the coffin.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> She was dressed in a white dress and her wedding veil.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> An array of mementos commemorating her extended family, friends and servants were laid in the coffin with her, at her request, by her doctor and dressers. One of Albert's dressing gowns was placed by her side, with a plaster cast of his hand, while a lock of John Brown's hair, along with a picture of him, was placed in her left hand concealed from the view of the family by a carefully positioned bunch of flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Items of jewellery placed on Victoria included the wedding ring of John Brown's mother, given to her by Brown in 1883.<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Her funeral was held on Saturday 2 February, in <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George's Chapel, Windsor Castle</a>, and after two days of lying-in-state, she was interred beside Prince Albert in <a href="/wiki/Frogmore_Mausoleum" class="mw-redirect" title="Frogmore Mausoleum">Frogmore Mausoleum</a> at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Great_Park" title="Windsor Great Park">Windsor Great Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With a reign of 63 years, seven months and two days, Victoria was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_in_Britain_by_length_of_reign" title="List of monarchs in Britain by length of reign">longest-reigning</a> British monarch and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_longest-reigning_monarchs" title="List of longest-reigning monarchs">longest-reigning</a> <a href="/wiki/Queen_regnant" title="Queen regnant">queen regnant</a> in world history until her great-great-granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> surpassed her on 9 September 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> She was the last monarch of Britain from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a>. Her son and successor Edward VII belonged to her husband's <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Cultural depictions of Queen Victoria">Cultural depictions of Queen Victoria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Victoria smiling" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG/170px-Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG/255px-Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG/340px-Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG 2x" data-file-width="727" data-file-height="975" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Her_Majesty%27s_Gracious_Smile_by_Charles_Knight.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Victoria amused. The remark "<a href="/wiki/Royal_we" title="Royal we">We</a> are not amused" is attributed to her but there is no direct evidence that she ever said it,<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> and she denied doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to one of her biographers, Giles St Aubyn, Victoria wrote an average of 2,500 words a day during her adult life.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> From July 1832 until just before her death, she kept a detailed <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria%27s_journals" title="Queen Victoria&#39;s journals">journal</a>, which eventually encompassed 122 volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> After Victoria's death, her youngest daughter, Princess Beatrice, was appointed her literary executor. Beatrice transcribed and edited the diaries covering Victoria's accession onwards, and burned the originals in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Despite this destruction, much of the diaries still exist. In addition to Beatrice's edited copy, <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Brett,_2nd_Viscount_Esher" title="Reginald Brett, 2nd Viscount Esher">Lord Esher</a> transcribed the volumes from 1832 to 1861 before Beatrice destroyed them.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Part of Victoria's extensive correspondence has been published in volumes edited by <a href="/wiki/A._C._Benson" title="A. C. Benson">A. C. Benson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hector_Bolitho" title="Hector Bolitho">Hector Bolitho</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Earle_Buckle" title="George Earle Buckle">George Earle Buckle</a>, Lord Esher, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Fulford" title="Roger Fulford">Roger Fulford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hough" title="Richard Hough">Richard Hough</a> among others.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Victoria was physically unprepossessing—she was stout, dowdy and only about five feet tall—but she succeeded in projecting a grand image.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> She experienced unpopularity during the first years of her widowhood, but was well liked during the 1880s and 1890s, when she embodied the empire as a benevolent matriarchal figure.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Only after the release of her diary and letters did the extent of her political influence become known to the wider public.<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_98-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Biographies of Victoria written before much of the primary material became available, such as <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Strachey" title="Lytton Strachey">Lytton Strachey</a>'s <i>Queen Victoria</i> of 1921, are now considered out of date.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> The biographies written by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Longford" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Longford">Elizabeth Longford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Woodham-Smith" title="Cecil Woodham-Smith">Cecil Woodham-Smith</a>, in 1964 and 1972 respectively, are still widely admired.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> They, and others, conclude that as a person Victoria was emotional, obstinate, honest, and straight-talking.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to popular belief, her staff and family recorded that Victoria "was immensely amused and roared with laughter" on many occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Through Victoria's reign, the gradual establishment of a modern <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> in Britain continued. Reforms of the voting system increased the power of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> at the expense of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> and the monarch.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> In 1867, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a> wrote that the monarch only retained "the right to be consulted, the right to encourage, and the right to warn".<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> As Victoria's monarchy became more symbolic than political, it placed a strong emphasis on morality and family values, in contrast to the sexual, financial and personal scandals that had been associated with previous members of the House of Hanover and which had discredited the monarchy. The concept of the "family monarchy", with which the burgeoning middle classes could identify, was solidified.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg/290px-Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg/435px-Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg/580px-Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2252" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Memorial_Kolkata_panorama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Memorial_(India)" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria Memorial (India)">Victoria Memorial</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a>, India</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Bronze statue of winged victory mounted on a marble four-sided base with a marble figure on each side" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG/170px-Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG/255px-Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG/340px-Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2144" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Memorial_London.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Memorial_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria Memorial (London)">Victoria Memorial</a> in front of <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> was erected as part of the remodelling of the façade of the Palace a decade after her death.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants_and_haemophilia">Descendants and haemophilia</span></h3>
<p>Victoria's links with Europe's royal families earned her the nickname "the grandmother of Europe".<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Of the 42 <a href="/wiki/Grandchildren_of_Victoria_and_Albert" class="mw-redirect" title="Grandchildren of Victoria and Albert">grandchildren of Victoria and Albert</a>, 34 survived to adulthood. Their living descendants include <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>; <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" title="Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh">Prince Philip, Duke of Edinburgh</a>; <a href="/wiki/Harald_V_of_Norway" title="Harald V of Norway">Harald V of Norway</a>; <a href="/wiki/Carl_XVI_Gustaf_of_Sweden" title="Carl XVI Gustaf of Sweden">Carl XVI Gustaf of Sweden</a>; <a href="/wiki/Margrethe_II_of_Denmark" title="Margrethe II of Denmark">Margrethe II of Denmark</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Felipe_VI_of_Spain" title="Felipe VI of Spain">Felipe VI of Spain</a>.
</p><p>Victoria's youngest son, Leopold, was affected by the blood-clotting disease <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia_B" title="Haemophilia B">haemophilia B</a> and at least two of her five daughters, Alice and Beatrice, were carriers. <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia_in_European_royalty" title="Haemophilia in European royalty">Royal haemophiliacs</a> descended from Victoria included her great-grandsons, <a href="/wiki/Alexei_Nikolaevich,_Tsarevich_of_Russia" title="Alexei Nikolaevich, Tsarevich of Russia">Alexei Nikolaevich, Tsarevich of Russia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Alfonso,_Prince_of_Asturias_(1907%E2%80%931938)" title="Alfonso, Prince of Asturias (1907–1938)">Alfonso, Prince of Asturias</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Infante_Gonzalo_of_Spain" title="Infante Gonzalo of Spain">Infante Gonzalo of Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> The presence of the disease in Victoria's descendants, but not in her ancestors, led to <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_of_Queen_Victoria" title="Legitimacy of Queen Victoria">modern speculation that her true father was not the Duke of Kent</a>, but a haemophiliac.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> There is no documentary evidence of a haemophiliac in connection with Victoria's mother, and as male carriers always suffer the disease, even if such a man had existed he would have been seriously ill.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> It is more likely that the mutation arose spontaneously because Victoria's father was over 50 at the time of her conception and haemophilia arises more frequently in the children of older fathers.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Spontaneous mutations account for about a third of cases.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Namesakes">Namesakes</span></h3>
<p>Around the world, places and memorials are dedicated to her, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Commonwealth nations">Commonwealth nations</a>. <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_named_after_Queen_Victoria" title="List of places named after Queen Victoria">Places named after her</a> include <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Africa's largest lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Falls" title="Victoria Falls">Victoria Falls</a>, the capitals of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Victoria,_British_Columbia" title="Victoria, British Columbia">Victoria</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Regina,_Saskatchewan" title="Regina, Saskatchewan">Regina</a>), two Australian states (<a href="/wiki/Victoria_(Australia)" title="Victoria (Australia)">Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Seychelles" title="Victoria, Seychelles">capital</a> of the island nation of <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross" title="Victoria Cross">Victoria Cross</a> was introduced in 1856 to reward acts of valour during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> and it remains the highest British, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross_(Canada)" title="Victoria Cross (Canada)">Canadian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross_for_Australia" title="Victoria Cross for Australia">Australian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross_for_New_Zealand" title="Victoria Cross for New Zealand">New Zealand</a> award for bravery. <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Day_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria Day (Canada)">Victoria Day</a> is a Canadian statutory holiday and a <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Day_(Scotland)" title="Victoria Day (Scotland)">local public holiday in parts of Scotland</a> celebrated on the last Monday before or on 24 May (Queen Victoria's birthday).
</p>
<h2><span id="Titles.2C_styles.2C_honours_and_arms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles,_styles,_honours_and_arms">Titles, styles, honours and arms</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles_and_styles">Titles and styles</span></h3>
<ul><li>24 May 1819&#160;– 20 June 1837: <i>Her Royal Highness</i> Princess Alexandrina Victoria of Kent</li>
<li>20 June 1837&#160;– 22 January 1901: <i>Her Majesty</i> The Queen</li></ul>
<p>At the end of her reign, the Queen's full <a href="/wiki/Style_of_the_British_sovereign#Styles_of_British_sovereigns" title="Style of the British sovereign">style and title</a> were: "Her Majesty Victoria, by the Grace of God, of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> Queen, <a href="/wiki/Defender_of_the_Faith" title="Defender of the Faith">Defender of the Faith</a>, Empress of India."<sup id="cite_ref-1900_Whitaker&#39;s_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1900_Whitaker&#39;s-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span></h3>
<ul><li>Founder and Sovereign of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Star_of_India" title="Order of the Star of India">Order of the Star of India</a> – 1861<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Founder and Sovereign of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Order_of_Victoria_and_Albert" title="Royal Order of Victoria and Albert">Royal Order of Victoria and Albert</a> – 1861<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Founder and Sovereign of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Crown_of_India" title="Order of the Crown of India">Order of the Crown of India</a> – 1878<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Founder and Sovereign of the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Order" title="Distinguished Service Order">Distinguished Service Order</a> – 1886<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Founder and Sovereign of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victorian_Order" title="Royal Victorian Order">Royal Victorian Order</a> – 1896<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arms">Arms</span></h3>
<p>As Sovereign, Victoria used the <a href="/wiki/Royal_coat_of_arms_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom">royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom</a>. Before her accession, she received no grant of arms. As she could not succeed to the throne of Hanover, her arms did not carry the Hanoverian symbols that were used by her immediate predecessors. Her arms have been borne by all of her successors on the throne.
</p><p>Outside Scotland, the <a href="/wiki/Blazon" title="Blazon">blazon</a> for the shield—also used on the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Standard_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal Standard of the United Kingdom">Royal Standard</a>—is: <a href="/wiki/Quartering_(heraldry)" title="Quartering (heraldry)">Quarterly</a>: I and IV, <a href="/wiki/Gules" title="Gules">Gules</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Lion_(heraldry)" title="Lion (heraldry)">lions</a> <a href="/wiki/Passant_guardant" class="mw-redirect" title="Passant guardant">passant guardant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pale_(heraldry)" title="Pale (heraldry)">pale</a> <a href="/wiki/Or_(heraldry)" title="Or (heraldry)">Or</a> (<a href="/wiki/Royal_Arms_of_England" title="Royal Arms of England">for England</a>); II, Or, a lion <a href="/wiki/Rampant" class="mw-redirect" title="Rampant">rampant</a> within a double <a href="/wiki/Tressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Tressure">tressure</a> flory-counter-flory Gules (<a href="/wiki/Royal_coat_of_arms_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal coat of arms of Scotland">for Scotland</a>); III, <a href="/wiki/Azure_(heraldry)" title="Azure (heraldry)">Azure</a>, a harp Or stringed <a href="/wiki/Argent" title="Argent">Argent</a> (<a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Ireland" title="Coat of arms of Ireland">for Ireland</a>). In Scotland, the first and fourth quarters are occupied by the Scottish lion, and the second by the English lions. The <a href="/wiki/Crest_(heraldry)" title="Crest (heraldry)">crests</a>, mottoes, and <a href="/wiki/The_Lion_and_the_Unicorn" title="The Lion and the Unicorn">supporters</a> also differ in and outside Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table border="0" style="margin:auto; width:50%;">

<tbody><tr>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestry">Ancestry</span></h2>

The Queen had turned the family gift into an Order in memory of her late husband, with its date of foundation her twenty-second wedding anniversary, 10 February 1861.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Badge+of+the+Order+of+Victoria+and+Albert+%28first+class%29+c.+1860-2&amp;rft.pub=Royal+Collection+Trust&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.royalcollection.org.uk%2Fcollection%2F442015%2Fbadge-of-the-order-of-victoria-and-albert-first-class&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AQueen+Victoria" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-237"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-237">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGazette24539" class="citation magazine"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.thegazette.co.uk/London/issue/24539/page/113">"No. 24539"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_London_Gazette" title="The London Gazette">The London Gazette</a></i>. 4 January 1878. p.&#160;113.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+London+Gazette&amp;rft.atitle=No.+24539&amp;rft.pages=113&amp;rft.date=1878-01-04&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.thegazette.co.uk%2FLondon%2Fissue%2F24539%2Fpage%2F113&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AQueen+Victoria" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-238"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-238">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGazette25641" class="citation magazine"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.thegazette.co.uk/London/issue/25641/page/5385">"No. 25641"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_London_Gazette" title="The London Gazette">The London Gazette</a></i>. 9 November 1886. pp.&#160;5385–5386.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+London+Gazette&amp;rft.atitle=No.+25641&amp;rft.pages=5385-5386&amp;rft.date=1886-11-09&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.thegazette.co.uk%2FLondon%2Fissue%2F25641%2Fpage%2F5385&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AQueen+Victoria" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
